VII
THE SHIFT TO PERSUASION

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

177

231

23

Introduction to Motions and Appeals

§23.1 Introduction to Persuasion Through Motions and Appeals
As a lawyer, you will use legal persuasion to convince judges to make decisions that favor your clients.
You will do this primarily through motions and appeals, procedures that allow you to put a request before
the court and get an answer. While motions and appeals often have an oral component, the lion’s share of
legal persuasion takes place in writing. Whether you are writing a motion memorandum or an appellate brief,
the ability to persuade centers on three skills: developing a persuasive theory (Chapter 24), telling the
client’s story (Chapter 25), and developing persuasive arguments (Chapter 26). This chapter will provide a
general overview of how motions and appeals work.

§23.1.1 What Happens During a Motion
A motion is the mechanism lawyers use to ask the court to take an action. The party making the motions
requests the court to issue an order. An order is the court’s command that something happen, such as
requiring the opposing party to comply with a discovery request. The court can also issue a judgment ending
the case, such as by granting a motion for summary judgment. The party making the request “moves,” or
files a motion, and the other party can oppose the motion.
Motions can be filed at any stage of litigation after a complaint has been filed, both at the trial court and
during appeal. You can file a motion to request something small, like extending a deadline, to something big,
like getting an entire case dismissed. Some motions are controlled by laws like the Rules of
232
Evidence or the Rules of Civil Procedure, and others are completely within the court’s discretion. You
might file many different motions in the process of litigating one law suit. Chapter 27 identifies many of
those motions.
Some motions are made orally, by asking a judge in open court. The vast majority, however, are made in
writing, by filing the request with the court. The party requesting the motion can file an accompanying
memorandum of law in support of the motion, and the opposing party can file a memorandum of law in
opposition to the motion. In some courts, these are called briefs. Every court has local rules governing what
form the motion and briefs should take, and identifying exactly what needs to be filed, so you should always
check the local rules when preparing a motion. Chapter 28 describes writing for motions in more detail.
After the motion is filed and the opposition has responded, the judge might hold a hearing if more
evidence is needed, or an oral argument to hear more directly from the lawyers. You can request an oral
argument if you think it will help to present your argument directly to the judge and answer questions, but in
most courts, the judge is not obligated to grant your request. As a result, many motions are decided based
only on the written documents, which is why it is so important for them to be well written and persuasive.
The judge grants or denies the motion and issues an order identifying what should happen next. This also
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

178

varies by local practice, but the majority of the time orders are proposed by the parties as a part of filing the
motion. Occasionally the judge will write a judicial opinion explaining the reason for the decision, but much
of the time the judge just issues the order and the case proceeds according to the outcome of the motion.

§23.1.2 What Happens During an Appeal
In an appeal, you ask an appellate court to affirm or reverse the outcome of a decision by the trial court.
For the most part, you can only appeal decisions that resolve an entire case or fundamentally affect the
outcome. For example, orders on motions to compel discovery or exclude evidence cannot be appealed until
the trial is over. A judgment — or in equity, a decree — is a decision that terminates a lawsuit and decides
the parties’ rights and obligations. The judgment might award damages or grant an injunction, for example.
A jury verdict also resolves the parties’ rights and obligations in a case. Judgments, verdicts, and some but
not all orders can be appealed.
The primary job of appellate courts is to correct errors made in the trial court, but appellate courts also
create precedent clarifying existing law and, in cases of first impression, making new law. In jurisdictions
with a two-tiered appellate court system, the intermediate appellate court focuses primarily on error
correction, while the highest appellate courts mainly focus on making and clarifying law.
233
In intermediate appellate court, appeals are typically decided by smaller panels of judges, rather than all of
the judges in that court. For example, U.S. Courts of Appeals decisions are made by panels of three judges.
In rare cases, a party that has lost before a panel may persuade the full court en banc to review the panel’s
decision. In the U.S. Supreme Court and in the highest court of every state, all the judges meet together to
hear and decide appeals.
Similar to motions practice, appellate practice varies from court to court and depends on the jurisdiction’s
rules of appellate procedure, but these are the significant steps in most appeals:
First, the party seeking the appeal (called the appellant or petitioner) files whatever document is required
by law to begin the appeal. In some courts, the appellant can appeal “as of right,” which means the court
must hear the appeal if the losing party has filed the appropriate documentation in a timely manner. In these
jurisdictions, the document is a simple notice of appeal, declaring that the losing party is pursuing its right to
have the case reviewed. Other courts, such as U.S. Supreme Court, have the discretion to refuse to hear an
appeal. In that situation, the appellant must petition and file a brief, arguing to the court that there are
important reasons to correct the error below or clarify important matters of law and policy.
Once the appeal is underway, the record (all the documents and evidence presented at the trial court) must
be transmitted from the court below to the court above. This can be simple and speedy, if the record is
electronic or where the appeal is from an order issued in the early stages of litigation, such as a motion to
dismiss a complaint. Most appeals, however, arise after more extensive litigation. In those cases, the
preparation and transmittal of the record can delay matters for months and add great expense to the cost of
the appeal, especially in many state jurisdictions that do not maintain electronic records.
In addition to the full record, some courts require the parties to compile an abbreviated version of the
record called the joint appendix or the record appendix. Because the full record can be so extensive, the joint
appendix allows the judges to more easily locate the record facts that are most relevant for resolving the
case. In jurisdictions without electronic records, the full record can consist of multiple volumes and be
extremely cumbersome.
The next step is drafting the briefs. If you are the appellant/petitioner, you file the opening brief, setting
out the arguments that lead to a result in your client’s favor. If you are the appellee/respondent, you file a
response, presenting the arguments for why the court should leave the lower court decision in place. The
appellant can file a reply.1 In novel cases or cases that present an issue of jurisprudential significance, the
court may allow other parties, known as amici, to file briefs arguing in support of one party or the other.
Each of
234
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

179

these briefs is an opportunity for persuasive writing. Chapters 29-31 explain appellate brief writing.
Once all the briefs have been filed, you may have an oral argument. As with motions, many appeals are
decided based exclusively on the briefs. When a court holds oral argument, each lawyer has the opportunity
to present their arguments and answer the judges’ questions. Oral argument is the lawyer’s only chance to
speak directly with the judges about the problems and issues raised by the appeal. For more on oral
argument, see Chapter 32.
After oral argument, the judges confer and discuss the merits of the appeal. One judge is selected to write
the court’s opinion. In some courts, the assignment is made by chance rotation, but in others it is made by
the presiding judge. In the U.S. Supreme Court, the assignment is made by the most senior judge among the
majority. The assigned judge drafts an opinion and circulates it to the other judges, who might suggest
changes or might draft and circulate concurring or dissenting opinions of their own. In routine appeals, the
draft majority opinion is often quickly approved, and concurrences and dissents may be held to a minimum.
But in more complex cases, views can change, and an opinion originally written as a dissent might be
transformed into the court’s opinion, while the original majority draft is converted into a dissent.

§23.2 The Roles of the Brief and of Oral Argument
The brief and oral argument each play important, yet different roles in persuading a court to rule on a
motion or appeal.
Because many cases are decided based on the briefs, what you write can play a critical role in influencing
the judge’s decision. In a brief, you can tell the client’s story and make the client’s arguments in detail. You
can present complex legal analysis with detailed citation to authority. A successful brief not only persuades
the judge that your client should win, but it can also be used as a manual, explaining to the judge exactly
how to make the decision and how to justify it in an opinion. A judge may use the brief to prepare for oral
argument, to prepare for the conference with other judges, to come to a decision about the case, and to write
the opinion.
Oral argument is not the place to present detailed legal analysis, but it can do two things better than a brief
can. First, in oral argument you can more immediately motivate the court by focusing on the most important
ideas — the few facts, rules, and policies — that make your case compelling. Second, in oral argument you
can try to discover, through the court’s questions, each judge’s doubts. And you can explain on the spot
exactly why those doubts shouldn’t prevent a ruling in your favor. Oral argument, in fact, is your only
opportunity to learn directly from the judges the problems they have with your arguments. But oral argument
also has a significant disadvantage. Oral argument
235
lasts only a few minutes, and memories of it can fade. The brief, on the other hand, has permanence. It’s
always among the judges’ working materials, and it “speaks from the time it is filed and continues through
oral argument, conference, and opinion writing.”2
Detail is communicated best in writing, which can be studied. But the spontaneity of conversation
encourages dialogue and lends itself to the broad sweep of underlying ideas.

§23.3 How Judges Read Briefs
How does a judge read a brief? The answer may vary considerably from judge to judge, but the following
is typical:
Usually I first read both parties’ statements of the questions presented; then I read the appellant’s statement of the
general nature of the controversy. Then I look at his outline of argument to see what points he makes. Then I look at
the appellee’s outline of argument to see what he is going to do in reply. Then I go to the joint appendix to see what
the trial court or the administrative agency did. Then I read the appellant’s statement of the facts and the appellee’s
statement. Thereafter I examine the two briefs one point at a time, first the appellant’s and then the appellee’s, on

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

180

the first point; then both briefs on the second point, etc. If the point is an obvious one, or if one side or the other
seems to be wholly without strength on it, I do not spend too much time on that point in my first study. On the
really contested points I study both sides, read the cases, and, if facts are critical check the record references. The
briefs on the critical points are often reread and reread.3

Other judges might read the parts of a brief in a different sequence — perhaps reading the point headings
before anything else — and a given judge might vary the sequence from case to case.
As a backdrop, consider how busy most judges are. Depending on the court, over the course of a month,
an appellate judge might hear oral arguments and confer with colleagues on several dozen appeals, and trial
court judges may be overseeing cases in the hundreds. For each motion or appeal, the judge will have to read
at least two briefs and in multiparty cases, a half-dozen briefs or more, together with portions of the record.
Trial court judges must issue orders and, where necessary, draft opinions on their own (or with the help of
the clerks who work for them). Appellate court judges will have to write opinions in a proportion of the
appeals before the court. On a five-judge court,
236
for example, each judge is assigned one-fifth of the majority opinions. In addition, the judges may also
write concurring or dissenting opinions in additional cases.
Appellate judges will also have to read opinions drafted by other judges and at times will write
memoranda to colleagues suggesting changes in those opinions. And trial and appellate judges will spend a
fair amount of time reading some of the authorities which are cited in all these briefs and draft opinions.
With all this work, the typical judge would find it a luxury to spend as much as an hour reading the average
brief. The time available is often no more than half an hour per brief. That’s why briefs must be carefully
written to persuade while demanding the least possible time and effort from the reader. Judges spend more
time on briefs where the appeal raises deeply troubling issues than on briefs in more routine cases. And a
judge who writes an opinion might read the briefs more thoroughly than one who does not.
As the brief-writer, the following considerations about your audience and their approach to reading should
guide you:
First, your brief will likely be read by multiple readers. Most judges have law clerks or research attorneys
who assist judges by studying the briefs and conducting independent legal research. They will read your
brief and make recommendations to the judge. In appellate courts, all of the judges deciding the case will
read your brief—that could be a panel of three or an en banc court of more than twenty. Opposing counsel
will also read your brief and develop arguments in response, as you will with theirs.
Second, a brief is read for specific purposes. Whether judge or clerk or opposing counsel, they are reading
to learn about the case and make decisions. Judges may read to prepare for oral argument, and again
afterward to decide how to vote. A judge will reread various portions of the brief several times while writing
the opinion, looking for the detail needed to justify and explain the decision. And all along the way, the
judges will be assisted by law clerks who check up on the details of the brief while the judges focus on the
broader principles. Each segment of the brief must be written to satisfy all of these purposes.
Third, briefs — like memos — aren’t read from beginning to end at a single sitting. They’re read in
chunks, at different times, depending on the needs of the reader. (You probably read an appliance or
automobile owner’s manual in pretty much the same way, and a brief is a manual for making a decision.)
Finally, a brief must be easy to navigate. A judge will go first to a part of the brief that reveals the
fundamental issues and your theory on each issue. A judge ought to be able to find that material plainly set
out in multiple places. In an appellate brief, for example, those places are: the point headings and
subheadings (collected in the Table of Contents); the Introduction or Questions Presented; the Summary of
Argument; and the Statement of the Case.
237
In a motion brief, it might be the Preliminary Statement or Introduction; the Statement of Facts; and the
point headings. Each judge has a favorite starting place. Not only do you have no way of knowing when you

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

181

write the brief where a given judge prefers to begin, but you’re writing for several judges and must
accommodate them all. Thus, you must draft these components so that each can separately be a
self-sufficient and self-explanatory entry point for any reader.
1. In most law school classes, both sides will turn in their briefs at the same time because the structure of the school
year does not leave time for back and forth filing.
2. Herbert Funk Goodrich, “A Case on Appeal — A Judge’s View,” in A Case on Appeal 10-1 (1967).
3. E. Barrett Prettyman, Some Observations Concerning Appellate Advocacy, 39 Va. L. Rev. 285, 296 (1953).

239

24

Developing a Persuasive Theory of the

Case
§24.1 Strategic Thinking
Luck is the residue of design.
— Branch Rickey

Every aspect of persuasive legal writing should be the result of a deliberate choice. You should have, and
be able to articulate, good reasons for the decisions you make in your writing. That includes decisions about
structure (What arguments or facts should I begin with?), vocabulary (What should I call the key people and
things?), and space (How much space should I devote to particular facts or arguments?). Making these
decisions requires careful thought and planning. For each choice you make, ask yourself how it will help you
win your case.
In post-mortems of your work, a supervising lawyer or a teacher might ask you a litany of questions about
strategy. You should ask yourself these questions before your supervisor does:
What was your goal?
What was your strategy?
What other possible strategies did you consider and reject?
For each rejected strategy, why was it inferior to the one you did choose?
What led you to believe that the strategy you chose would actually achieve the ultimate goal?
Did you do all the things necessary to execute the strategy you chose?
240

§24.2 Theories: Of the Case, of the Motion, of the Appeal
The first step in articulating a goal and planning a strategy is to figure out what your theory of the case is.
The theory of the case combines the relevant law and the key facts, and gives an overarching rationale for
why you win. It is the essence of your case. One commentator describes it as a “succinct, general description
of your version of what happened and why” coupled with a “characterization that paints your client in a
positive light.”1 A good theory has logical and emotional appeal: it is “persuasive both to the mind and to the
heart.”2
Persuading is selling, and judges have accurately been described as “professional buyers of ideas.”3 You
are selling your theory to the judge with your brief (and, if you have one, in your oral argument).
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

183

On appeal, the theory of the case may be referred to as theory of the appeal. On motion, it may be referred
to as theory of the motion. Although these terms refer to a similar concept, lawyers do consider the level of
court and procedural posture when crafting their theories.
Specifically, when crafting a theory, lawyers consider that a trial court is a place of routine, and trial
judges (generally) are not in a position to make grand changes in the law. Trial judges do not like to be
overturned by appellate courts and they try to make decisions that will avoid that result. While trial judges
certainly consider policy, they are likely to respond positively to a theory that shows how the case outcome
conforms to existing law in their jurisdiction.
Appellate judges, on the other hand, are conscious of their responsibility to see the bigger picture because
they are making law for an entire circuit (in the federal realm) or the entire state. The rules they announce
must apply to a wide range of factual circumstances. This is especially true in cases of first impression where
little or no mandatory law exists. Appellate courts are also mindful of precedent, but are equally concerned
with the broader social implications of the law.

§24.3 Developing a Theory: Be Open to Possibility
When Savana Redding approached lawyers in the case of Safford v. Redding4 and told them that her
school had strip-searched her to look for drugs, a lawyer who lacked the skill of theory design might have
said, “I’m sorry Savana, but
241
public school students have limited rights under the Constitution and school districts have broad latitude.
I’m afraid you are unlikely to prevail in a lawsuit.”
But Savana’s actual lawyers were very skilled and they brainstormed. Was this really a case analogous to
the search of a student’s backpack (which was the major Supreme Court case on this issue)? Might the law
draw a different line when a body search is at issue? Were drugs even found? What kind of drugs were at
issue?
Notice the technique. First, think about fairness and justice. The law isn’t always fair and just, and it
doesn’t always help people who have been hurt, but as lawyers we can explore if any possibility exists to
right a wrong. So, explore the possibilities. Be open to your emotions here. If it doesn’t seem fair, maybe
there is a legal argument there. Second, open doors to additional factual possibilities. Ask the questions.
Third, find out how the law treats those possibilities. You shouldn’t let your feelings of fairness outweigh
your reading of the law, but your feelings should inform how you approach the law.

§24.4 Developing a Theory: Process
Legal research and a solid understanding of your factual record guides the growth of your theory. At the
same time, your theory also guides the course of the research. That circular process of crafting a theory,
looking carefully at your record and researching the law, brainstorming a theory, researching more, and then
reevaluating a theory is a critical lawyering skill. Your first version of your theory of the case may be quite
rudimentary and may develop or change as you learn more about the law.
Ask what the case is really about. Finish this sentence: “This is a case about . . .”. Is it a case about a
broken promise? An overzealous school principal? A rogue FBI agent who ignores the protections of the
Constitution? What do you see as the human conflict in the case?
Ask what will matter to the court. The human conflict is, of course, not the end of your inquiry. You
must think about the legal issues. The law doesn’t right all wrongs. Certain kinds of suffering — for good
reasons or bad — have no effect whatsoever on the typical judge. Do research to see what is significant to a
legal audience.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

184

Make a list of your strengths and weaknesses. Taking into account what you know about the law, what
are your best facts? You might want to review the chapter on persuasive facts (Chapter 25). What facts that
favor your client’s position are both legally relevant and have a high emotional appeal? Those are your best
facts. What are your worst facts? (Your worst facts are usually the best facts for the other side.)
242
Consider your opponent’s likely theory. Finally, it can help to develop your adversary’s most likely
theory while creating your own. If you look at the case as your adversary will see it, and if you
hypothetically work up a theory that your adversary might argue, you’ll be able to identify the weaknesses in
your theory. Otherwise, you will look at the controversy one-sidedly, and your theories will reflect wishful
thinking and be too unidimensional to withstand attack.
Articulate a very short story or headline. Using what you have learned about the law, your best facts,
your worst facts, and the human elements of the case, what is the best story you can tell? This story should
be short: a headline, a tweet, or a “six word story.”5
Look at these two Questions Presented from the Safford v. Redding case. Can you tell what the litigants’
theories of the case are? Try to formulate theories for both sides. Then look at the list of possible theories of
the case. Notice how theories of the case can vary significantly.
School District:
Whether the Fourth Amendment prohibits
public school officials from conducting a
search of a student suspected of possessing
and distributing drugs on campus in
violation of school policy.

Savana:
Whether it is reasonable to subject a 13-year-old girl to the
indignity of a strip search based on an unreliable accusation
that she previously possessed ibuprofen and no information
that she possessed ibuprofen in her undergarments at the
time of the search.

Possible Theories for the School District:
1. The health and safety of all middle school students must outweigh the privacy interests of one.
2. School officials must be able to safeguard students by searching students who might be distributing
drugs to other students.
3. It was reasonable to search the student because the school had evidence she was going to sell
dangerous drugs to other students.
Possible Theories for Savana:
1. A strip search of a minor for a legal, common painkiller is way beyond what is acceptable in our
society.
243
2. The Fourth Amendment is a safeguard against overzealous school officials who would strip a
13-year-old girl to search for a legal drug.
3. The School District had no evidence to justify such an invasive, harmful search of a young girl.

§24.5 How to Evaluate Your Theory
To figure out whether your theory stands a significant chance of persuading a court, ask yourself the
following questions.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

185

24-A Does your theory present a positive, assertive account of why your client should win?
Does your theory have a solid basis in fact? Make sure your theory is fully supported by your facts

24-B and isn’t marred by hyperbole or bias.

Does your theory have a solid basis in law? Are your interpretations of the statutes and cases

24-C reasonable? When a court examines your legal arguments, will the court be persuaded?

Does your theory pass the “straight-face” test? In other words, is it plausible? Would you be

24-D comfortable stating it to a judge’s face in chambers during a pretrial meeting or during oral

argument? All else being equal, a simple theory is more down-to-earth than a complex one. The most easily
sold theories are those that are based on easily believable interpretations of the evidence and the authorities;
that would lead to reasonable results; that don’t ask a judge or jury to believe that people have behaved in
improbable ways; and that ask for narrow decisions rather than earth-shaking ones. (A judge would much
rather find that your client is not guilty on the facts than hold that the statute defining the crime is
unconstitutional.)
Does your theory “[e]xplain why people acted in the way they did”?6 A theory is effective when

24-E it rings true for the reader. If a theory assumes that the actors behave differently than a reader would

expect in that situation, the theory isn’t persuasive unless it includes a compelling reason for the difference.
Theories that impute deceit to otherwise objective witnesses, for example, are less attractive than those that
suggest honest mistakes.
244
Does your theory account for unfavorable facts? Your theory should be a positive assertion about

24-F why your client prevails. It also must be broad enough to explain the evidence against you. All the

School District theories of the case noted above, for example, are some version of “we had to keep students
safe” or “we had to stop her from dealing drugs.” Note that the School District theories do not highlight the
strip search, because that is a very bad fact for them, but when confronted by that fact, their theory still
holds: student health and safety trumps one student’s right to privacy.
Exercise
Escape from Prison? (Developing a Theory)
Orville Bradwyn is charged with the crime of escape from prison. The state’s sole witness was Benjamin
Tunmeyer, a prison guard, who testified as follows.
Q

A:

Q

Please tell the court what you observed and did at 6:30 in the evening on the sixth of July.
I was checking prisoners in the dinner line. Prisoners are required to be in there at that time, and
any prisoner who hasn’t shown up for dinner is considered missing. The defendant didn’t appear. I
then checked his cell. Some material had been put in his bed, bunched up so that it looked like
somebody was asleep there. His radio had been left on. But he was gone.
What did you do?
We searched the grounds outside the prison. We didn’t find the defendant there, so we searched
inside the prison — first the perimeter, and then the inside of buildings and containers where
someone might hide. We finally found him in the laundry room at 7:39 P.M.
What did he have with him?

How is the hospital’s laundry transported to and from the prison?
By truck. The hospital’s truck brings it in the morning and picks it up at about 8 P.M.
Are prisoners permitted in the laundry room in the evening?
No prisoner is allowed in that room after 5 P.M. Hiding in one of the hospital’s laundry bags is an
obvious way to escape from the prison.
What precautions are taken to prevent that?
At 5 P.M., a guard makes sure all prisoners assigned to work in the laundry have left, and then the

makes sure it has only laundry in it. Then he locks up the room and locks another door on the

Letters from his family, personal photographs, letters from his lawyer, an address book.
Before dinner, prisoners are free to move about outside their cells; aren’t they?
Yes.
And the same is true after dinner, isn’t it?
Until 7:30.

After this testimony, the prosecution rested. Before any defense witnesses testified, Bradwyn moved to
dismiss on the ground that the prosecution had presented insufficient evidence to convict.
Develop two theories that satisfy the criteria in §24.7. One theory should support Bradwyn and his
motion. The other should support the prosecution and oppose the motion. Use the method outlined in this
chapter.
The relevant statute and cases interpreting it appear below:
CRIMINAL CODE § 745
If any person committed to prison shall break and escape therefrom or shall escape or leave
without authority any building, camp, or any place whatsoever in which he is placed or to which
he is directed to go or in which he is allowed to be, he shall be deemed guilty of an escape and
shall be
246
punished by imprisonment for a term not to exceed five years, to commence immediately upon
the expiration of the term of his previous sentence.
STATE v. HORSTMAN
The crime of escape is established by proof that the defendant was confined in a prison and
escaped from such confinement or departed without authority from a place to which she or he was
duly assigned. Unauthorized departure is the gravamen of the offense.
STATE v. CAHILL
While incarcerated, the defendant was placed in solitary confinement for fighting with another
prisoner. A guard inadvertently left the cell door unlocked. The defendant got out and was
apprehended on top of the prison wall.
The defendant argues that the evidence does not prove that he committed the crime of escape
because there is no evidence that he escaped from the custody of the Department of Prisons. He
argues that, at most, he is guilty of the lesser crime of attempted escape.
The crime of escape was complete, however, when the defendant got out of his cell. The crime
can be committed without leaving the prison as a whole. It is enough that the defendant left a place
where he was confined within the prison.
STATE v. LIGGETT
The defendant was incarcerated and assigned to work in the prison shop manufacturing auto
license plates. On the day in question, the defendant was reported absent from his shift in the
license plate shop. After a prolonged search, he was found inside a machine in the prison cannery,
using a pillow, and reading a novel.
The evidence does not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the defendant committed the crime
of escape. He failed to report for work in one part of the prison and, without authorization, spent
the time in another part. That might violate prison rules and merit internal prison discipline, but it
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

is not the crime of escape.

247

25

Telling Your Client’s Story in a Statement

of the Case (or Facts)
[M]ost contentions of law are won or lost on the facts.
— Justice Robert Jackson, Associate
Justice U.S. Supreme Court

How you present the facts of your client’s case is critically important. People think in story form and we
remember stories vividly. Think of how much easier it is to remember a legal case with vivid facts as
opposed to sections of the Tax Code. Many lawyers will tell you that they would rather have bad law than
bad facts. Bad law we can get around. Bad facts tend to stick. This chapter explains how to tell your client’s
story in a persuasive way, highlighting the good facts and minimizing the bad.
The first thing to keep in mind is that presenting your client’s facts is about telling a story. Your fact
statement should be clear and well written, but it should also have a clear emotional, intuitive appeal. It
should meet three basic goals: (1) to motivate the judge to act in your favor; (2) to communicate your
fundamental facts, including who the parties in conflict are, to apprise the judge of the basic issue; and (3) to
highlight favorable and neutralize unfavorable facts.
Before reading this chapter, you might want to first review Chapter 10 on fact analysis and peruse the
Statements of Facts in the Appendix F motion memo and the Statements of the Case in the Appendices G
and H appellate briefs.
248

§25.1 The Basics
Fact statements in briefs have different names in different courts. In the U.S. Supreme Court and the
Federal Courts of Appeals, the fact section of the brief is called the Statement of the Case. Statement of Facts
is also a commonly used name, especially in trial courts (see Appendix F motion memo).
Your first step in telling your client’s story is to read your record. The legal record is the definitive
compilation of the evidence in a case.
One of the first things a lawyer does when starting to write a brief is to read the record carefully and make
a timeline of the facts, identifying what happened and when. Once you have the basic outline of what
happened, you can begin to analyze which facts are good for the client (and which are bad). These are the
building blocks for the story.
For the most part, every statement you make in your statement of facts must have a citation to the record.
With rare exceptions, if something is not in your record, it generally cannot be included, even if your client
swears to you that it is true. In other words, if it isn’t in the record, for your purposes (and the court’s) it
doesn’t exist as a fact.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

190

If a fact appears in your Argument, it should be in your facts. Period.
Your Statement of Facts should be only facts — no legal argument or inferences. “Joe ran a red light” is a
fact (if it is in your record). “Joe drove carelessly” is an inference. “Joe was negligent” is a legal argument.
Only facts that are drawn from the record belong in the facts. Everything else goes in the Argument. The
only exceptions to this are inferences that witnesses drew, which if they are in the record testimony are facts.
You can also state inferences that your adversary won’t contest.
The absence of a fact from the record can itself be a fact. You can say things like “Nothing in the record
shows that . . .” or “No witnesses identified the defendant.”

§25.2 The Basic Tools of Persuasive Storytelling
If you cannot argue or make inferences in your statement of facts, how will you persuade? To begin,
consider three basic building blocks of persuasion: structure (in what order to present things), space (how
much space to devote), and language (what to call things). In your facts statement, you will state your facts
in a carefully chosen order, using deliberately chosen language, and devoting the most space to your good
facts and the least to your bad ones.
Once you have your timeline and you’ve done some legal research so that you know which facts the law
considers relevant, begin to assess your facts as good or bad for your client’s position. It is helpful to have a
working theory of the case (Chapter 24) at this point, though you may revise it after evaluating your facts
more closely.
In evaluating your facts, consider both legal relevance and emotional power. A legally relevant fact is one
that has the potential to change the legal outcome
249
of a case; some lawyers refer to these facts as outcome determinative. If the fact were not present, or were
the opposite, the legal outcome would be different. Emotional power refers to the intuitive, emotional pull of
a fact. Some facts are legally relevant but don’t have much emotional pull, like the date a contract was
signed or the manufacturer of a faulty airbag.
The most important facts, and the ones around which you likely based your theory of the case, are usually
those with high legal relevance and high emotional power. The good facts that have the highest relevance
and emotional power are the ones you will emphasize. The bad facts that have the highest relevance and
emotional power are those you have to think carefully about how to handle.

§25.3 The Opening
Your reader should know immediately who your client is when she reads the very first sentence of your
facts. Think about where your story should start. Most students default to chronological order, but this is not
always the most persuasive use of structure. Consider your theory of the case and what event is most
important to your theory, and start there. For example, in a case about criminal assault in a bar, the
prosecution might begin the story with the first punch the defendant threw at the victim, or even the victim’s
medical treatment (how many staples, loss of blood). The defense, on the other hand, might go back in time
to when the victim entered the bar and started drinking. Or, might choose to begin with what the victim said
to the defendant before the fight began.
Often, lawyers begin the facts with a short, theory-heavy “preamble” that introduces the case and tells the
judge why the case is before the court. The preamble, like the theory of the case, finishes the statement “This
case is about . . .” But skilled legal advocates avoid that hackneyed phrase. Consider this opening from Ricci
v. DeStefano, a case about an affirmative action policy by the City of New Haven:
In 2003, New Haven sought to fill command vacancies in its fire department. Petitioners qualified for promotion
under a race-blind, merit-selection process mandated by local law but were denied the positions by the city because
of their race and racial disparities in the examinations’ outcome. City officials refused to promote the successful

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

191

candidates and left the vacancies unfilled, intending to repeat the competition with the aim of awarding a higher
proportion of the promotions to minority candidates. [Footnote omitted.]

There can be no question which side wrote this opening. That is so even though the paragraph contains
only facts — few adjectives and adverbs, no overstatement, just facts. Notice the juxtaposition of “qualified
for promotion . . . based on a merit-selection process mandated by local law” and “refused to promote” and
“left the vacancies unfilled.” Petitioners’ theory of the case is
250
loud and clear: they were qualified but were denied the positions based on their race. The opening
simultaneously apprises the court of the overarching impetus of the lawsuit and persuades the court of the
rightness of Petitioners’ position.

§25.4 Tips for Effective Storytelling
Using Structure. Put good facts at points of emphasis. Most readers notice information that occurs at the
beginning of a point of emphasis and remember things that are stated last. That means that your good facts
should be at the beginning and end of: your statement (macro organization), headings (if you use them), and
paragraphs (micro organization).
Using Space. The majority of space in your statement should be good facts. Unpack the details of your
good facts so that your reader has a rich, thick description of the good facts. Overuse of adjectives and
adverbs is a common novice error in the statement of facts. These words are not descriptive — they tell as
opposed to show. They also compress facts instead of expanding them. Consider the following example:
Telling
(adjectives and
adverbs in
bold):

Showing (better):

Defendant paid a
strongman to
violently
terrorize the
family of the
man who owed
him money.
Defendant knew
that the man
would use
unsavory
collection
practices.

Over a period of several months, defendant met with Robbie Mutch, a man with several
convictions for arson and assault. Mutch had approached defendant and offered to “go
after” Jessup, who owed defendant money. Over the course of these meetings, defendant
agreed to “hire” Mutch to get his money back. Mutch then followed Jessup until he was
alone and broke his arm with a pipe, saying “it’s time to pay.” When this did not
immediately work, he also went to the school attended by Jessup’s children, two girls
ages 7 and 9, and gave the girls “notes” to pass on to their father. The notes said things
like “look how easy it was to get to your kids” and “next time you’ll pry this note from
her beaten body.” Mutch told defendant that he was going to do these things, and
defendant paid him to do them.

The adverbs and adjectives in the “telling” example do not help the court understand what happened. They
are the writer’s editorializing. If you asked ten lawyers what “unsavory” practices are, you would get ten
answers. Moreover, “violently terrorize” is a loaded term that can encompass a wide range of behavior.
Instead, show the court what happened and let the court make the value judgments.
251

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

192

This second example is much more memorable than the first. When you are done with a first draft of your
facts, consider reviewing them to check for adverbs and adjectives. Did you overuse them? Are there places
where you could substitute detail for an adjective or adverb to better effect?
Another good way to check your facts for persuasiveness is to count sentences. How many sentences are
good facts? How many are bad? In most cases, there should be far more sentences devoted to good facts than
bad.
Using Space, Part 2. Make sure you do not clutter your fact statement with extraneous information. As
you go through your story, ask yourself whether each fact you include is necessary either because it is
relevant, emotionally affecting, or important to the context. Sometimes lawyers call this the “so what”
question. Are some of your included facts “so what” facts, meaning if you took them out it would change
absolutely nothing about the case or the reader’s understanding? Get rid of them. They force the reader to
wade through a lot of clutter to get to the gems.
Compare these:

In the passage on the left, the following are all clutter: the date, the address, the precise times, the booking

Using Language. Consider carefully what you want to call people, events, and things. Choose words that
resonate with your theory. Use vivid, memorable language for your good facts. Consider the effect of words,
the images. In terms of people, think about the difference between calling someone a “defendant” versus
“Mr. Harris.” Or calling people “rioters” as opposed to “protestors.” Or calling the entity in a criminal case
“the prosecution” versus “the People of Indiana” versus “the State.”
The Safford v. Redding case is a good example of the importance of what to call things or events. In that
case, recall, a middle school student was searched on suspicion that she possessed prescription-strength
ibuprofen. The School District refers to “drugs” multiple times in its brief, occasionally referring to “OTC”
drugs (over the counter) or “IBU 4000s.” Savana’s brief refers to the drugs almost exclusively as
“ibuprofen.”

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

193

§25.5 Dealing with Bad Facts
Every case has bad facts — those facts that are damaging to your client’s position. Although the
professional rules of ethics impose upon lawyers a duty of candor, that duty does not require lawyers to
disclose bad facts to the tribunal in a litigation setting.1 The decision to include bad facts is most often a
strategic one and depends on many factors.
If the damaging fact is one that the other side is likely to disclose and rely on, or the court is likely to learn
of it, good advocacy counsels in favor of confronting that fact and trying to neutralize it. Lawyers sometimes
call this “drawing the sting” or “inoculation.”2 Confronting bad facts has two advantages. First, it makes you
look credible and reasonable in the eyes of the court, the benefit of which cannot be overstated. Second, by
confronting the bad fact, you take full advantage of the opportunity to characterize that fact (hence, “drawing
the sting”).
There are several strategies for handling bad facts in written advocacy, including:
Structure: Juxtapose Next to a Favorable Fact That Explains, Counterbalances, or Justifies It. This
is often, but not always, done through the “Even though [this], [that]” or “Although [this], [that]”
formulation. In a case about the legality of a search, the brief for the United States might juxtapose the fact
of the search (a bad fact for them) with the reason for it (a good fact): “TSA agents made
253
the determination to examine Mr. Ballard’s luggage only after Jessie, a drug-sniffing beagle, had signaled
that Mr. Ballard’s suitcase contained contraband.”
Language: Passive Voice, Euphemizing. Your legal writing professor has probably told you that passive
voice should be avoided because it is wordy and creates ambiguity. That is true. However, good advocates
learn to control the passive voice, using it effectively to deflect blame. Think about the difference between
“Agents of the TSA patted her down and then emptied her suitcases of all their contents” with “She and her
belongings were looked over for contraband.” The passive hides the actors and obscures power, agency, and
authority. Passive makes it seem like things “just happened.” This can be a valuable tool for the advocate.
You can use it, but use it wisely and control your use.
Similarly, you can euphemize. The same way you used vivid, colorful language to describe your good
facts, you can use less “charged” language for your bad facts. Think about the difference between “search”
and “look over” or “inspect.” All of these words have different connotations. Like everything else in the
brief, the words you choose to describe the key components of the case should be carefully chosen.
“Bury” the bad facts structurally and devote less space to them. Although hiding bad facts by
avoiding points of emphasis and using generic language is often effective, using only these methods to deal
with bad facts is like sticking your head in the sand. You can’t hide or ignore facts that your opponent is
going to emphasize. Use this method, but use it in conjunction with one of the other methods described for
full effect.

§25.6 The Procedural History
So far, we have been talking exclusively about the facts related to the dispute between the parties — what
conflict happened between the litigants that brought them to court. But the fact statement has another
important component: the procedural history. You are likely familiar with the concept of procedural history
from briefing cases for your other classes. In an appeal brief, the procedural history covers what happened
before the appeal was filed. In a motion brief, it covers what happened leading up to the filing of the motions
at issue. If it is advantageous to the client, the procedural history might include detail about the substance of
what happened below. For example, if the appellate brief writer won in the trial court, he may elaborate on
the trial court’s reasoning.
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/21/2022 10:39 PM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

194

Your legal writing professor will talk about what kind of information to include in the procedural history.
It will vary depending on context, including how critical procedure is to the resolution of the case (e.g., Is
there a procedural issue?) and how complicated the procedural posture is.
254

§25.7 How to Evaluate Your Statement of Facts
Does your opening paragraph focus on your best core facts and/or make clear your theory of the

25-A case?
25-B
25-C
25-D
25-E

Have you used structure, space, and language to tell the story most persuasively?
Have you emphasized favorable facts?
Have you neutralized unfavorable facts?
Have you humanized your client?
Have you made careful decisions about what to include and exclude? Is the statement of facts

25-F streamlined, with little “so what” clutter?

Have you checked your statement of facts for inferences and legal argument? Have you checked for

25-G overuse of adjectives or adverbs?
25-H

Have you included a procedural history?

§25.8 Story Ethics
As you know, the rules of professional conduct forbid a lawyer from “[k]nowingly mak[ing] a false
statement of material fact or law” to a court.3 But even if the rules were silent, you should never be
misleading or false with the court. As a lawyer, your word is your bond and your reputation will precede
you. It is also simply poor advocacy. And it will backfire because the court will be angry and will likely hold
it against your client. It is never a good day when your adversary accuses you of misrepresenting the facts in
open court and the judge says, “Well, let’s look at the record” and you cannot find the support for your
statement.
Maybe this seems obvious to you and you think you would never do such a dishonest thing. But being in
advocacy mode can have a significant impact on your worldview. The authors have found that we sometimes
misremembered facts — coincidentally always in favor of our clients — and had to look back carefully at
our records after drafting the statement to be sure we were accurate. We weren’t trying to mislead, but we
sometimes found ourselves doing so inadvertently because we felt so passionately for our clients.
255
Also, the line between appropriate advocacy and overstatement is not an easy or bright line. We all know
a lie when we see one, but it is harder to know whether a euphemism we use for a bad fact is legitimate
advocacy or misleading. The best way to guard against this is (i) scrupulous citation to the record; (ii)
thinking about making the statement in open court (Does it pass the straight face test?) and/or bouncing your
characterizations off your peers or your professor; and (iii) checking your fact statement for inappropriate
inferences and editorializing.

At trial, a climatologist testified about the climate in

below freezing, but there is no water to freeze. Spring
and fall temperatures approximate summer
temperatures elsewhere. July is the hottest month, with
an average high of about 116° and an average

Average annual rainfall is about 2½ inches, and the

The climate in Death Valley is one of the

Exercise II
Escape from Prison? (Rewriting Fact Statements)
Orville Bradwyn is being tried for attempted escape from prison. Below is a Statement of Facts for the
prosecution, followed by one for the defense. (Citations to the record have been omitted.)
Rewrite each Statement.
For the prosecution:
The defendant is a prisoner at the Simmonsville Penitentiary. On July 6, he was not in the dinner line
where he was required to be, and Sgt. Tunmeyer, the prosecution’s sole witness, organized a search for
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

196

him. According to Sgt. Tunmeyer, no prisoner is allowed in the laundry room after 5 P.M., and the
defendant was found in the laundry room at 7:39 P.M. The reason for this rule is that the laundry room is
an obvious place from which to escape. The prison does the laundry of the state hospital nearby, and
hospital trucks pick up hospital laundry directly from the prison laundry room. At 5 P.M., Sgt.
Tunmeyer checked the laundry room thoroughly, did not see anyone, and subsequently locked the door.
Even though the defendant had been assigned to work in the laundry, he was not on duty when he was
found in the laundry. Before and after dinner, prisoners are free to move about outside their cells. When
the defendant’s cell was checked, many of his personal belongings were found. In fact, the defendant
had a reputation for being tidy and was drying all of his clothes when found in the laundry at 7:39 P.M.
However, when his cell was checked his radio had been left on, and some material had been left under
the covers of his bed to make it look like someone was asleep there.
For the defendant:
Mr. Bradwyn is an inmate at the Simmonsville Penitentiary. At this prison, inmates are free to move
about outside the cells before and after dinner, which is from 6:00 to 7:00 P.M. The prisoners are
counted each day in the dinner line, and when Mr. Bradwyn was not present, he was considered
missing. Prisoners are not allowed in the laundry after 5 P.M. because the laundry room provides an
obvious escape route on the truck the state hospital sends at 8 P.M. each night to pick up its laundry. Sgt.
Tunmeyer testified that he checked the laundry at 5 P.M. and locked it up without seeing Mr. Bradwyn
there.
257
When Mr. Bradwyn was discovered to be absent from the dinner line, Sgt. Tunmeyer organized a
search and found him in the laundry room at 7:39 P.M., where Mr. Bradwyn was drying his prison
uniforms. There was no testimony about how Mr. Bradwyn could have entered the laundry room, which
Sgt. Tunmeyer claims to have locked. Mr. Bradwyn is one of the prisoners assigned to work in the
laundry. Sgt. Tunmeyer admits to past quarrels with Mr. Bradwyn, and that Mr. Bradwyn had filed a
complaint against him for beating him. He also admitted that Mr. Bradwyn is an unusually tidy person
who does not like other people to touch his things, and that at 7:39 P.M. all of Mr. Bradwyn’s clothing
was in a dryer.
The state has not introduced any evidence that Mr. Bradwyn was not on duty earlier in the day. Nor
has it introduced any evidence that Mr. Bradwyn could not have been locked in the laundry room
accidentally by Sgt. Tunmeyer at 5 P.M.
