26

Developing Persuasive Arguments

§26.1 What Is an Argument?
An argument is a group of ideas arranged logically to convince a reader or listener to do a particular
thing or to adopt a particular belief.
That doesn’t include hostile comments between drivers after a fender bender in a parking lot. The drivers
are really venting anger, neither of them having any hope of persuading the other. Nor does it include
aggressive expression of one’s opinion on politics, fashion, or sports. Many people are eager to state their
opinions so that you know what they think — and not with the intent of changing your mind. But if they are
truly trying to persuade you, they’re making a genuine argument.
Uncapitalized, the word argument means a contention designed to persuade. Capitalized, an Argument is
the largest portion of a motion memo or appellate brief. An Argument might include many separate
individual arguments.
In designing an argument, your first question should be “What will make the reader or listener want to
agree with me?” A good argument will affect the audience. It leads readers or listeners through reasoning so
convincingly that, at the end, they are pleased to be persuaded.
Most disputes are two-sided in the sense that each side can make credible arguments, and it takes real
work for the judge or jury to choose between them. Here is an example of opposing arguments from the
parties’ briefs in Lawrence v. Texas:1
260
Section 21.06 puts the State of Texas inside its
citizens’ homes, policing the details of their most
intimate and private physical behavior and dictating
with whom they may share a profound part of
adulthood. Texas has enacted and enforced a criminal
law that takes away — from same-sex couples only —
the freedom to make their own decisions, based upon
their own values and relationships, about the forms of
private, consensual sexual intimacy they will engage
in or refrain from. The State defends this law only by
saying the majority wants it so. Texas asserts a power
of the majority to free itself from state dictates about
private, consensual sexual choices, while using the
criminal law to condemn and limit the choices of a
minority. This law and its application to Petitioners
violate both the guarantee of equal protection and

The States of the Union have historically
prohibited a wide variety of extramarital sexual
conduct, a legal tradition which is utterly
inconsistent with any recognition of a
constitutionally protected liberty interest in
engaging in any form of sexual conduct with
whomever one chooses. Nothing in this Court’s
“substantive due process” jurisprudence
supports recognition of a constitutional right to
engage in sexual misconduct outside the
venerable institution of marriage. This Court
should adhere to its previous holding on this
issue in Bowers v. Hardwick, and it should
reaffirm that the personal liberties protected by
the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth
Amendment from State regulation are limited to

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

198

fundamental liberties safeguarded by the Fourteenth
Amendment.

those “so rooted in the traditions and conscience
of our people as to be ranked as fundamental.”

Your job is to make your arguments more appealing than your adversary’s, or to make your adversary’s
arguments weaker by pointing out their flaws, or — preferably — to do both of these things. This chapter
explains how.

§26.2 What Judges Expect from Written Argumentation
Judges are busy readers who view any assertion skeptically and who must make many decisions in short
periods of time. Thus, they need complete but concise arguments that can be quickly understood.
261
Judges are not experts in all areas of the law. In most courts, judges cannot specialize in particular areas of
substantive law: they must decide any case you bring before them. Unless a case turns on parts of the law
about which a judge has thought deeply lately, the judge will depend on you to explain what the law is and
how it governs the case. And a judge knows nothing at all about the facts of a case except for what can be
learned through the lawyers and their evidence.
Your job is to teach and persuade at the same time. Think of a motion memo or appellate brief as a
manual on how to make a particular decision (and on how to write the opinion that justifies that decision). A
lawyer who can show the court how the decision should be made, by laying out all the steps of logic, stands
a much better chance of winning.

§26.3 Types of Legal Arguments
A carefully prepared, carefully stated, lawyer-like written argument is a work of art and a joy forever.
— E. Barrett Prettyman

Good legal arguments are reasonable and accurate, appear reliable, and make your client’s victory appear
inevitable — either because the higher courts will reverse any other result, or because it’s the only right
thing to do, or both. The feeling of inevitability is a judge’s selling point. It’s reached by laying out for the
judge every step of logic so that your conclusion becomes irresistible as the argument proceeds. It is
accomplished by making an assertion and then backing it up with evidence (from your record) and authority.

§26.3.1 Motivating and Justifying Arguments
Arguments can fall into two basic categories: motivating and justifying.
Motivating arguments make the judge want to rule for your client. They are not so much about legal proof
as emotion or intuition. Aristotle called this pathos. Pathos gives your arguments an intuitive, gut appeal.
They make the judge believe your position is right in a grand sense of achieving justice — not just legally
correct.
This is how you might express the motivating argument in the Appendix F motion memo:
The Defendant did not take basic precautions to make sure he did not file a claim barred by res judicata.

262

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

199

Note the way the motion memo reinforces the idea of the defendant as a bad actor by emphasizing his failure
to act and highlighting how simple the precautions would have been. This motivates the reader to see the
defendant in a negative light.
Justifying arguments are those based in law, fact, and proof. A justifying argument shows proof of a
conclusion of law according to the paradigm explained in Chapters 11-14. In a justifying argument, you
identify the controlling rule of law and show that the rule’s application to the case facts lead to a positive
result for your client.
A simplified version of a justifying argument in the Appendix F motion memo would be:
The Defendant has not met his burden of proof by providing evidence to support his bona fide error affirmative
defense.

This argument relies on providing concrete legal standards and applying them to the case facts.
A good Argument section in a motion memo or appellate brief will include both types of argument. A
motivating argument alone isn’t enough because even a motivated judge isn’t supposed to act without a solid
legal justification. Have you ever seen an opinion without citations to authority? No. That is why motivating
arguments are not enough. A justifying argument is also often not enough, particularly if the law can be
interpreted in more than one reasonable way. When a judge is given a choice between two conflicting
justifying arguments, each of which is reasonable, a motivating argument can affect which choice the judge
makes.

§26.3.2 Policy Arguments
Policy arguments are arguments about the effect that the rule you are advocating will have in the future.
Policy arguments answer questions such as: if the court does as you request, how would the law in the future
treat facts that are similar to — but not exactly the same as — yours? What would be the practical effects in
the courts, in the economy, and in society as a whole? Why is the rule you advocate better than the one your
adversary urges?
Policy arguments can be highly motivating, especially in a close case. While there is no standard structure,
here is a sound approach for making policy arguments:
1. Begin by identifying one or more public policies that a decision in your favor would further. Tell the
court exactly which policy or policies you want the court to be guided by.
2. Then persuade the court that the policy or policies you’ve identified are valuable. Your adversary
will urge competing policies. Show that yours
263
are more important. But it’s not enough just to say yours are worth more; prove it with argument.
3. Finally, show exactly how a decision in your favor will further the policy or policies you’ve
identified. Don’t assume that the court will understand how. Explain it specifically.
Prove policy with authority. Judicial opinions often include policy and are a good source of authority.
Common policy concerns addressed in cases include public health and welfare, enforceability of rules, ease
of administration, and the proper role of courts. Policy considerations may differ from state to state. In
Arizona, for example, public policy disfavors solutions that interfere with development of land for homes
and industry, while in Vermont policy prefers conservation, the environment, and preservation of
agriculture. Some states favor providing tort remedies even at some risk to judicial efficiency, although in
others the reverse is true.
If you lack legal authority, cite and explain nonlegal sources that show a genuine public need. For
example, if you argue that the courts should adopt strict tort rules assigning liability for contamination with
industrial chemicals, you can cite scientific studies showing the presence of PCBs and other toxic chemicals
in the food supply.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

200

When a court is being asked to clarify the interpretation of a statute, the court isn’t free to attach to the
statute any policy the judges like. Instead, the court must use the policy that the legislature adopted when it
enacted the statute. Sometimes that policy is expressed in the statute or the legislative history. If not, the
court, with your assistance, must figure out what policy the legislature probably adopted.
But also go beyond that and demonstrate that what you want will also produce the best result in future
cases, remembering that the decision will become precedent. If a court must choose between competing
rules, for example, show that the rule you urge is better than others. If you win, the decision will stand for
that rule.
Lawyers tend to introduce policy-based arguments with phrasings like the following:
This Court should reject the rule urged by the defendant because it would cause . . .
Automobile rental companies [or some other category of litigants] should bear the risk of loss because . . .
Not only is the order requested by the plaintiff not sanctioned by this state’s case law, but such an order would
violate public policy because . . .

Remember, however, that policy arguments are used to reinforce argument from authority. Only where
authority is unusually sparse should policy arguments play the predominant role in a theory.
264

§26.3.3 Brainstorming Arguments
Legal arguments come in many different forms. We’ll outline just a few here. Many of these are based on
Wilson Huhn’s The Five Types of Legal Argument.
Arguments from text. Common in statutory and constitutional issues, these arguments posit that a result
is mandated by the plain language of the legal text at issue. You have probably seen judges talk about the
“common meaning” of a word, or even use a dictionary to support what most people believe is the meaning
of a particular word or phrase. That’s argument from text.
Arguments from precedent and analogy. The most common legal argument, these posit that the court
should reach a result because courts have reached similar results in similar cases.
Arguments from intent. These are close cousins of policy arguments, discussed below. But instead of
focusing on general social good, these arguments posit that a result is required because that is what the law
intended to happen. You will often see these arguments in statutory or constitutional cases, but they also
crop up in common law. All laws have a reason or purpose for why they were passed or made. Judges are
meant to interpret and apply the law to serve those purposes. Arguments from intent state that the law’s
purpose is best accomplished by a decision in the advocate’s favor.
You will need authority to support your intent arguments. Sometimes a statute will be very clear about the
purpose it meant to serve. Other times, intent must be inferred (by you or the court) by looking at legislative
history or even sometimes newspaper articles about the law. For example, you can easily discern the purpose
of “Megan’s Law,” which required sex offenders to register in a state database, by looking at news accounts
surrounding the law.
Policy Arguments. As discussed above, these arguments posit that a ruling in favor of the advocate’s
position will result in a social good and/or a ruling against the advocate will result in a social evil.

§26.3.4 Selecting and Organizing Your Arguments
Macro Organization. First decide on the big picture — which arguments to include and in what order to
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

201

present them. You might be tempted to include every point you can think of to support your theory and
refute your adversary’s arguments. This approach can hurt more than help. Instead, focus sharply on your
strongest points. Develop them fully, and leave out the weak ones. As Holmes put it: “strike for the jugular
and let the rest go.”2
265
Include only those arguments which, in your judgment, have a reasonable chance of success. A
“reasonable chance” doesn’t mean certainty and, in a case where you have nothing better, it might not even
mean probability. To be worth making, however, a contention should have the potential to lead to a decision
in your favor. Lawyers often use the “straight face” test: If they would feel comfortable making the argument
to a judge’s face (say, during oral argument), then it is worth putting in the brief.
Once you have decided which arguments to include, consider the most effective way to organize them.
The general rule is strongest arguments first unless logic dictates otherwise.
For example, if your adversary must prove that a five-element test has been satisfied, and if you think that
your adversary’s proof is weakest on element number three, don’t argue the elements in the order in which
they’re listed in the controlling statute. Argue element three first because as far as you’re concerned, it’s the
controlling element. Your adversary will likely have a very different organization, for example, arguing the
elements in the order they appear or addressing those elements that are easier to satisfy before those that are
more challenging.
Sometimes, however, the logic of the dispute requires that the strongest material be delayed to avoid
confusing the court. Some arguments are simply hard to understand unless preceded by less punchy material.
In these situations, you must weigh your need for clarity against your need to show merit from the start.
Micro Organization. While justifying arguments can be organized through the paradigm formula because
they are proofs of a conclusion of law (see Chapters 11-14), motivating arguments are more often appeals to
a human sense of justice or pragmatic policy needs. Motivating arguments can be made at any point in the
Argument section of a motion memo or a brief, but they often work best at the very beginning of a section.
Here is an example of how to incorporate motivating arguments into the paradigm structure:
Conclusion: You can “beef up” the conclusion with additional sentences incorporating why your result is
fairer, more likely to work justice, or any other motivating argument. Note that the conclusion part of the
paradigm can be more than one sentence.
Rule: You can add material here about the intent of the rule and why your articulation of the rule best
serves the intent. You can also insert policy here, to show that your rule will best serve some public policy.
Rule proof: If the cases discuss intent or policy, include that in your discussion of the cases. You can add
nonlegal materials here to support what the cases say, particularly if the cases say it in a conclusory manner.
266
Application: Use the policy that you have raised in the first parts of the paradigm to apply your rule. Show
how the facts of your case directly implicate the intent or policy at issue.
Communicating Organization. Use “signals” to persuade and organize. Don’t let the judge grope for
clues about how your arguments relate to each other. Instead, use concrete language to help the judge see
your focus. Review how to use point headings (Chapter 30). Use paragraphing to signal a change in topic or
argument. Use thesis sentences to show the main point of the paragraph to the reader. And use transitional
words and phrases to show how your points are cumulative:
There are three reasons why . . . First, . . . Second, . . . And finally, . . .
Not only has the defendant violated . . . , but she has also . . .

These verbal and visual cues will make your Argument easier to follow and understand, which will enhance
persuasion.
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

202

§26.3.5 Stating Rules Clearly and Persuasively (Rule and Rule Proof)
Your argument must make clear the legal rule that supports the outcome you are advocating to the court.
That rule might not be exactly as stated in the cases you cite. In fact, the cases might enforce the rule without
stating it at all, and you might have to figure out what the rule is based on the court’s reasoning, particularly
the way it treats the facts.
State the rule in a way that leads to a decision in your client’s favor when it is applied to the facts. The
judge drafting an opinion will want it to be as convincing as possible to the parties, to the bar, to the public,
and to any higher court to which the decision could be appealed. That is a hard task where a gap in the law
must be filled. The judge who asks in oral argument in a gap-filling case, “Counselor, what rule would you
have us enforce?” really wants to know how — if the lawyer prevails — the court should word the second
component of the paradigm formula when it writes the opinion. Your goal should be to describe the rule in a
way that the court can adopt it without modification.
To convince a court to adopt a rule you are advocating, you need to provide sufficient authority to show
that the rule is legally justified. Consider the amount of citation and explanation a skeptical but busy judge
would need. Separate the authorities into categories based on how much influence they will have on the
outcome. Think in terms of cause and effect. If you had to make the judge’s decision, which authorities
would probably have an effect on you, even an effect adverse to your client’s position? Thesis sentences will
267
help you here — you should be able to articulate the goal of using a particular case or line of cases, and
that goal should be clearly stated in a thesis sentence. The motion memo in Appendix F provides a good
illustration of effective rule statements in the roadmap section (between headings I and A).
The amount of authority and the depth of explanation will depend on how much is needed to clarify the
issue involved, how disputed that issue is, and how important it is to your theory. At one extreme, an idea
may be so complex, so disputed, and so critical that it must be supported by a comprehensive explanation,
filling many pages, of major authorities. At the other extreme, if the court is apt to be satisfied with a mere
conclusory explanation, you should limit citation to one or, at the very most, two cases. (For example, notice
how the test for summary judgment is proved in the memo in Appendix F.)

§26.3.6 Working Carefully with the Facts (Fact Application)
A court rules for one party over another not merely because the law is abstractly favorable, but, more
importantly, because the law and facts combine favorably. After all the work of explaining the law, a
beginner might assume that the application to the facts is obvious, but it hardly ever is. Don’t assume that
merely mentioning the facts is enough: argue the facts. In other words, show the court exactly how the
determinative facts require the decision you seek. If you set up your rule persuasively, when you apply it to
the facts, it should seem clear and obvious that it leads to the outcome you seek.
The key to arguing the facts is to make sure nothing in the “fact application” is a pure statement of fact.
Instead, the sentences should connect law and fact. For example, if you are arguing that a defendant is liable
for reckless driving when he was driving the posted speed limit in the middle of a snowstorm, you might
state as the rule, “a driver is liable for maintaining an unsafe speed in disregard of adverse weather
conditions,” and then in applying that to the facts, go into detail about the conditions at the time of the
accident. A pure fact is “John drove through the snowstorm.” A combination of law and fact is “John
disregarded the snowstorm when he maintained his speed on the highway.”
Review your fact application for “so what” problems. A pure fact assertion such as “John drove through
the snowstorm” will make a legal reader say “so what” because the writer has not told the reader why John’s
unsafe speed matters legally.
There is more room for inference and vividness in the Argument fact application than in the Statement of
Facts, but restrain yourself from melodrama. Try to hit the line between being vivid and argumentative and
being overly dramatic. It’s the difference between a reader’s “whoa, that’s terrible” and a reader’s eyeroll.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

203

We wish we could give you an easy rule for this, but experienced lawyers struggle with this line. The best
way to learn is to try different methods and get feedback.
268

§26.4 How to Handle Adverse Authority and Arguments
Which authorities most strongly favor your adversary? (Make a list.) What is your adversary’s strongest
argument? Her second strongest argument? If you’re having a hard time answering these questions, you
haven’t begun to see the case as a whole.
Ignoring adverse authority won’t make it go away. It just gives opposing counsel the opportunity to
characterize it and you lose the opportunity to show why it doesn’t undermine your argument. Also, if you
ignore it and the court learns about it, be prepared for the court to be very angry and draw negative
conclusions about your credibility.
Dealing with Mandatory Adverse Authority. If the adverse authority is a statute, court rule, or
administrative regulation, you must show that the provision does not apply to your case, or that it applies in a
way that favors your client. Statutory language is almost always ambiguous and susceptible to multiple
interpretations, one of which could favor your client. Although it may seem tempting to argue that a statute
you don’t like is unconstitutional, courts rarely sustain such attacks. You should frontally attack a statute
only if there’s significant doubt — shared by respected lawyers — about its validity.
If the adverse authority is precedent, try to weaken it using the techniques described in Chapter 9.
Consider distinguishing the precedent, focusing on significant — and not merely coincidental — differences
between the precedent and your case. But be sure that these differences pass the “so what” test. The
differences on which you rely should be legally significant. Hypertechnical discrepancies and minor factual
variations won’t persuade because they seem arbitrary rather than a basis for a just decision. Another
approach might be to reconcile the precedent with your case, showing that — although the precedent seems
superficially adverse — its underlying policy would actually be furthered by the ruling you want from the
court. Still another approach is to attack the precedent head-on, challenging its validity on the grounds that it
is poorly reasoned or that changes in society or in public policy have made it unworkable. For reasons
similar to those outlined above about frontal challenges on statutes, this should be a last resort.
Adverse Authority That’s Not Mandatory. If the adverse authority is from outside your jurisdiction,
then you are much freer to criticize and urge the court to reject it. If a judge must choose between competing
out-of-state rules, she won’t be able to decide without rejecting at least some precedent as ill-founded. This
is where policy arguments can make a difference.
Make your own arguments before refuting opposing arguments. You’ll win more easily if the court’s
dominant impression is that you deserve to win,
269
rather than that your adversary deserves to lose. A defensive tone can undermine an otherwise worthwhile
argument. For that same reason, give adverse arguments only the space needed to dispatch them and no
more. The overall tone of your motion memo or appellate brief should be affirmative, not defensive.
Consider counting paragraphs to see how many are positive, affirmative arguments and how many rebut
arguments from the other side. Positive should greatly outweigh negative in most cases.
Beginners often have difficulty writing the transition and thesis sentences that introduce attacks on
opposing arguments. As with your thesis sentences in your positive argument, the thesis sentence
introducing your rebutting argument should encapsulate your response. For example:
The plaintiff misconstrues § 401(d)(1). Four other circuits have already decided that § 401(d)(1) provides for X
and not, as the plaintiff contends, for Y. [Follow with an analysis of the circuit cases.]

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

204

No appellate court has held to the contrary, and the few district court decisions cited by the plaintiff are all
distinguishable. [Follow with an analysis of the district court cases.]
The legislative history also demonstrates that Congress intended to provide for X and not for Y. [Follow with an
analysis of the legislative history.]

These opening sentences are written so that opposing counsel’s contention is surrounded by the writer’s
counter-contention and the beginning of the counter-contention’s proof. Avoid giving voice to the other
side’s argument by saying, “the plaintiff argues . . .” or “the defendant states that . . .”.
An “although” clause can be useful in thesis and transition sentences:
Although the House Judiciary Committee report states that its bill would have provided for Y, the Senate Judiciary
Committee actually drafted § 401(d)(1). Both that committee’s report and the conference committee report flatly
state that § 401(d)(1) provides for X.

§26.5 Argumentation Ethics
The rules of professional ethics place limits on what you’re permitted to do in argument.
First and most basically, you’re forbidden to “knowingly . . . make a false statement of fact or law” to a
court.3 The whole system of adjudication would break down if lawyers did not speak honestly to courts.
Second, you must inform a court of “legal authority in the controlling jurisdiction” that you know “to be
directly adverse to the position of [your] client
270
and not disclosed by opposing counsel.”4 You aren’t required to disclose out-of-state authority. You’re
required to disclose authority in your jurisdiction that’s “directly adverse” to your arguments if your
adversary hasn’t disclosed it.
Third, you may not advance a theory or argument that is “frivolous,” except that you may make a “good
faith argument for an extension, modification or reversal of existing law.”5 In a legal system like ours, where
“the law is not always clear and never is static,” the rules of ethics permit you to advance theories and
arguments that take advantage “of the law’s ambiguities and potential for change.”6 But a frivolous theory or
argument — one that stands little chance of being adopted by a court — is unfair to courts and to opposing
parties because it wastes their time, effort, and resources.
Separate court rules — procedural, rather than ethical in nature — also punish lawyers who make
frivolous arguments. In federal trial courts, for example, every “pleading, written motion, and other paper”
must be signed by a lawyer, whose signature certifies “that to the best of the [signer’s] knowledge,
information, and belief, formed after inquiry reasonable under the circumstances . . . the claims, defenses,
and other legal contentions are warranted by existing law or by a nonfrivolous argument for extending,
modifying, or reversing existing law or establishing new law.”7 Where that standard is violated, the court has
the power to impose monetary fines on the offending lawyer.8 Similar rules govern in appellate courts.9

§26.6 Evaluating Your Arguments
As you develop your arguments, consider whether you have included the elements that will make your
Argument section strong and persuasive.

26-A

Have you designed a compelling theory and backed it up with persuasive arguments?
Have you made both motivating arguments and justifying arguments and included policy arguments

26-B where relevant?

Have you limited your contentions to those that have a reasonable chance of persuading the court?

205

26-C 271

26-D
26-E
26-F
26-G
26-H
26-I
26-J

Have you organized to emphasize the ideas that are most likely to persuade?
Have you made your organization obvious?
Have you given the court a clear statement of the rule or rules on which the case turns?
Have you relied on an appropriate amount of authority with an appropriate amount of explanation?
Have you explained exactly and in detail how the law governs the facts?
Have you reinforced your theory with carefully chosen wording?
Have you confronted openly your weaknesses and your adversary’s strengths?
Have you enhanced your credibility through careful editing and through the appearance of your

26-K memo or brief?

Handling the Procedural Posture

§27.1 Why Procedural Postures Matter
The procedural posture is the procedural event or events — such as a motion — that places an issue before
the court. The procedure affects the type of decision a judge can make, and may also limit how the court can
view the facts, so a lawyer writing a motion memorandum or appellate brief must take the procedural
posture into account.
Each type of motion is governed by rules on how the motion is to be decided. For example, if you move
for summary judgment, you must satisfy the test for summary judgment, which requires you to accept your
opponent’s version of the facts as you make your legal arguments. You are saying, in other words, that even
if every fact relied upon by your opponent is true, you still win. In contrast, if you are moving to exclude an
expert witness from trial, you may be entitled to provide the court with your own evidence to support your
position. In an appeal, the appellate court may be limited to finding whether the trial court correctly followed
the procedure, rather than independently deciding the case.
Because the procedural posture plays such a significant role in shaping the arguments you can make in a
motion memorandum or appellate brief, identifying and understanding it should be your first step when
preparing to write.

§27.2 Types of Procedural Postures
Trial court motions fall into four very generalized categories: (1) motions that challenge the quality of an
adversary’s allegations (in a pleading); (2) other
274
motions that challenge the manner in which the litigation began; (3) motions that challenge the quality of
a party’s evidence; and (4) a large, catchall category of miscellaneous case management motions. When a
trial court’s decision is appealed, the case moves into yet another procedural posture, where the trial judge’s
decision is evaluated according to a standard of review.
Rules governing how motions are decided may differ from state to state. Each state and the federal court
system has its own rules of procedure. Check the rules that govern the jurisdiction for which you are writing
a memorandum or brief, as well as the precedent interpreting those rules. Always check for local rules from
the specific court where you will be filing the brief.

§27.2.1 Motions Challenging the Quality of a Party’s Allegations
In litigation, parties initiate legal claims through documents called pleadings. In a civil case, the plaintiff’s

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

207

complaint alleges violation of a legal principle and the facts that support that violation. The complaint must
allege facts that, if proven, would constitute a cause of action. The defendant’s pleading is called an
“answer.” If a defendant pleads a counterclaim or an affirmative defense in the answer, that answer must
allege facts that, if proven, would substantiate a counterclaim or affirmative defense. And in a criminal case,
the government’s indictment or information must allege facts that, if proven, would be a crime.
Because, at this stage in litigation, no evidence has been submitted, the facts mentioned in the pleadings
are called allegations. When ruling on motions attacking allegations, courts must generally assume that the
alleged facts are true, but will eventually have to be proven by evidence submitted to the court. One
exception is that an allegation is considered true and proved if the opposing party admits to it in its own
pleadings.
In a civil action, a defendant can move to dismiss a plaintiff’s complaint for failure to state a claim.
Because this motion tests the sufficiency of allegations (and nothing more), the record is limited to the four
corners of the complaint. Motions to dismiss are often called “so what” motions: “even if everything the
plaintiff alleges is true, so what?” The court must assume the plaintiff will be able to prove all of the
allegations and decide whether they meet the requirements of the legal claim. If the answer is yes, the case
will move forward to give the plaintiff the opportunity to prove the allegations. If the court strikes all the
claims pleaded in a complaint, the complaint itself is dismissed and the litigation is over unless the plaintiff
appeals, or can file an amended complaint with additional or reformulated allegations that would survive a
motion to dismiss.
Similarly, a plaintiff can move to dismiss a counterclaim or an affirmative defense pleaded in the
defendant’s answer. And in a criminal case a defendant can move to dismiss one or more counts in the
indictment or information, or the entire indictment or information.
275
In motions challenging a party’s allegations, you must be careful to avoid referring to facts as if they are
proven. For example, you would write “Plaintiff alleges Defendant was driving too fast” rather than
“Defendant was driving too fast.” If, however, the opposing party has admitted the fact, you may treat it as a
proven fact and refer to it accordingly.

§27.2.2 Motions Challenging Other Aspects of the Way in Which the Litigation Began
A defendant might move to dismiss an action on the ground that the court lacks personal or subject matter
jurisdiction, venue is improper, the summons did not include all the information required, the complaint was
improperly served, or that some persons who must be made parties have not been,1 and so on.
Like motions challenging the allegations, these seek dismissal of the action at the earliest stage of
litigation, before the defendant files an answer. These motions aren’t limited to the contents of the
complaint. If the defendant asserts that the summons was improperly served, for example, the court would
ignore the contents of the complaint and would instead hear testimony from the process server and from the
defendant about how the summons was delivered to the defendant.

§27.2.3 Motions Challenging the Quality of a Party’s Evidence
These include (1) motions for summary judgment; (2) motions for directed verdict (or in federal courts,
motions for judgment as a matter of law); and (3) motions for judgment notwithstanding the verdict (in
federal courts, renewed motions for judgment as a matter of law). In contrast to the motions testing
allegations in pleadings, these require the court to decide whether a party has carried a burden of production.
There are also (4) motions for a new trial, which are analytically different from the other three (explained
further near the end of this section).
The burden of production (often called the burden of going forward) means that the party seeking relief
can provide enough evidence that a rational fact finder — a judge or jury — could decide in that party’s
favor. The burden is defined by the relevant rules of procedure and by the case law interpreting those rules.
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

208

When you bring a motion to challenge the quality of the other party’s evidence, the judge applies those
standards to rule on the motion.
Although these motions are governed by different procedural rules, all three are decided according to
approximately the same logic: the motion should be granted if (1) the opposing party has failed to satisfy a
burden of production
276
and (2) the law is such that the movant is entitled to a favorable judgment. These motions require a judge
to apply the substantive law to the facts and determine whether the requirements of the claim can be
satisfied. For example, if you move for summary judgment in a products liability case, the judge will have to
decide if the facts in the record meet all of the elements of a products liability cause of action.
A party brings a motion for a new trial after a verdict has been rendered. A new trial can be granted on
two kinds of grounds: (1) the jury’s verdict is seriously tainted through procedural faults such as erroneously
admitted evidence or inaccurate jury instructions, or (2) the verdict was against the overwhelming weight of
the evidence. The first category obviously doesn’t test the quality of a party’s evidence. The second does, but
it does so by examining the extent to which a burden of persuasion (rather than a burden of production) has
been carried. A new trial isn’t granted merely because the judge would have come to a different verdict if
she had been the trier of fact. But it can be granted if the overwhelming weight of the evidence is on one side
of the case and the jury returned a verdict for the other side.

§27.2.4 Miscellaneous Case Management Motions
These are housekeeping motions, used to manage the progress of litigation, such as motions in discovery,
motions for preliminary injunctions, and suppression motions in criminal cases. What makes these motions
different from the ones you’ve just read about is that the granting of a management motion doesn’t terminate
the litigation; instead, management motions regulate the litigation’s progress.
For example, before trial, a criminal defendant who gave the police a confession might move to suppress
it, arguing that the police wrongfully obtained the statement by failing to inform him of his constitutional
right to remain silent. If the court grants the motion, the statement cannot be used as evidence at trial.
Case management motions cannot be appealed, though the trial judge’s ruling on these motions can be
challenged after the full case has been resolved.

§27.2.5 Appeal
On appeal, a standard of review is applied to the decision below. Standards of review are explained in §29
.3.

§27.3 Writing in a Procedural Posture
You will understand this section more easily if you read the Appendix F motion memo first.
277
Because much of law school is focused on studying the substantive law of torts, property, and so on, you
might tend to view issues in the abstract (“should the plaintiff win?”). But judges are constrained by the
procedural context in which issues are raised.
If, for example, a plaintiff moves for summary judgment, the judge can’t just decide in the abstract
whether the plaintiff should win the case. Instead, the judge must decide whether the parties disagree about

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

209

the material facts and whether the plaintiff is entitled to judgment as a matter of law (the test for summary
judgment) — in other words, whether it is possible for a jury to rule in the plaintiff’s favor. Compare the
following Questions Presented:
Is a painting contractor liable for negligence if he power-sands large amounts of lead paint from the exterior of a
building and thus spreads lead paint debris over most of an adjacent preschool?
Is plaintiff preschool entitled to summary judgment for its negligence claim against a painting contractor if it is
undisputed that the contractor power-sanded large amounts of lead paint from the exterior of a building and thus
spread lead paint debris over the adjacent plaintiff preschool?

The first question is stated abstractly without reference to the procedural posture. It will not make as much
sense to the court as the second. The italicized words in the second example aren’t throwaway jargon. They
reflect the test for summary judgment and are the core of the decision the court must make.
Similarly, on appeal the question isn’t whether the appellant should have won in the trial court, but instead
whether the trial court’s ruling was error as defined by the applicable standard of review (see §29.3). See
Chapter 30 (Point Headings and Subheadings) for how to incorporate the procedural posture and appellate
standard into your headings.
Because the procedural posture and the rules governing it control the way the judge will make the
decision, you must show the judge how to decide within those procedural rules. How can you do that in
writing?
First, remember that in a motion or appeal the threshold rule is procedural. It isn’t the rule of substantive
law that provides the remedy sued for. In a trial court, the threshold rules are the rules that govern how the
motion is to be decided. On appeal, the threshold rules are the ones that govern the trial court’s decision plus
the appellate court’s standard of review. So you must develop your argument to be consistent with the
limitations of those rules.
For example, the motion memo in Appendix F has been submitted by plaintiff Beatrice Russell in
opposition to a motion for a summary judgment. In deciding the motion, the court will evaluate the record in
terms of the elements of the test for summary judgment: whether there is a genuine dispute about a material
fact and whether, on the undisputed facts, the movant is entitled to judgment as a matter of law. That test is
invoked very early in Russell’s Argument.
278
Second, some procedural tests incorporate the underlying substantive rules on which the litigation as a
whole is based (causes of action, affirmative defenses, and so on). In the test for a summary judgment, that
happens through the element requiring entitlement to judgment as a matter of law. In the test for a
preliminary injunction, it’s done through the element requiring likelihood of success on the merits. In these
situations, you make those substantive legal arguments within the framework of the procedural requirements.
Third, organize your paradigm variations around the procedural test. See Chapters 11-14. If the procedural
test incorporates a substantive test, the substantive test operates as a sub-rule. To see how that’s done, read
carefully the Argument in Appendix F, which includes several sub-arguments inside the organization of an
umbrella structure. The umbrella structure — set out under the major heading in roman numeral I — is built
on the test for a summary judgment. Because the second summary judgment element (entitlement to
judgment as a matter of law) incorporates a rule of substantive law (the test for the bona fide error defense),
proof of the second summary judgment element includes all of the arguments that show why the defense is
not available. Note the way each argument ties back to why the defendant failed to carry his burden and how
that affects summary judgment. This tracks the sequence a judge will follow in deciding whether to grant or
deny the motion.
Finally, be thoughtful about providing authority for the procedural test. Most procedural tests are so
commonly invoked that judges know them by heart. A conclusory explanation (see §12.2) is often sufficient
for rule proof. (And that’s all that was needed at the beginning of Beatrice Russell’s Argument in Appendix
F. Can you find the sentence and cite where it happens?) But often attorneys provide more. This may be
necessary if the parties disagree about the appropriate formulation of the procedural rule. In other cases,

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

210

attorneys might provide more as part of their persuasive rhetoric. A lawyer requesting summary judgment
might cite a case that shows how easy it is to grant such a motion, while a lawyer opposing summary
judgment might look for citations that say that summary judgment is an extraordinary remedy because it
removes the case from the hands of a jury (or other fact finder) and should be granted sparingly.

§27.4 Researching to Account for Your Case’s Procedural Posture
You’re looking for two kinds of rules:
First, you are, of course, searching for the rules that govern the substance of the controversy — definitions
of causes of action, crimes, affirmative defenses,
279
and other forms of rights and obligations like the ones you’ve already learned. This is by far the larger
research task in nearly all instances.
Second, you’re looking for the procedural rules that govern how the court’s decision is to be made. Some
examples are —
1. rules setting out the tests for granting various motions (“Summary judgment is appropriate if there is
no genuine issue of material fact and if the moving party is entitled to judgment as a matter of law”);
2. rules controlling how the court must evaluate the record before it on the motion to be decided (“In
deciding a motion for summary judgment, the court views the evidence in the light most favorable to
the party opposing the motion”); and
3. if an appeal has been taken, the rule defining the standard of review in the appellate court (“On
appeal, a grant of summary judgment is reviewed de novo”).
You can save a lot of research time if you first identify the type of motion involved and then look in the
procedural statutes and court rules, as well as in the cases for procedural rules that govern the motion’s
disposition. For any given motion, you’ll probably find part of the procedural law in a statute or court rule
and the rest in interpretive case law.
If you find the procedural rules in a procedural statute or court rule, the language may be subtle, but it
won’t be terribly hard to recognize. The section heading alone will usually announce that you have arrived at
the right place — for example, “Rule 56. Summary Judgment.”
In case law, when a court mentions the rules governing how a motion is to be decided, it usually does so
immediately after reciting the facts and immediately before beginning the legal analysis. That is because the
procedural rules are a threshold through which the court must pass in order to begin the analysis. This is
typical of the kind of language you’ll find:
Summary judgment should be granted when the moving party shows that there is no genuine issue as to any
material fact and that the moving party is entitled to judgment as a matter of law. [Citation omitted]. In other words,
the time and expense of the parties and the court should not be wasted on a trial when there are no material facts in
dispute, one party is entitled to judgment on those facts, and thus there is nothing to try. In deciding a motion for
summary judgment, all reasonable inferences are construed in favor of the nonmoving party. [Citation omitted]. The
party opposing the motion for summary judgment must “submit evidentiary materials that set forth specific facts
showing that there is a genuine issue for trial.” [Citation omitted]. “The nonmoving party must do more than simply
show that there is some metaphysical doubt as to the material facts.”

280
[Citation omitted]. Summary judgment is properly entered against a party “who fails to make a showing
sufficient to establish the existence of an element essential to the party’s case, and on which that party will bear the
burden of proof at trial.” [Citation omitted].2

The first sentence is the test that must be satisfied before the motion can be granted. The rest are some of

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

211

the rules that govern how the court is to evaluate the record before it on this particular type of motion.
1. See Rule 12(b) of the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure.
2. Sommers v. Target Corp., 382 F.Supp.3d 842, 846 (E.D. Wis. 2019).

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

212

281

28

Motion Memoranda

§28.1 Motion Memorandum Format
When a motion is made, each party may submit a memorandum of law presenting an argument to the
court about the decision it should make. A defendant moving for an order dismissing a complaint, for
example, submits a document that might be titled “Memorandum in Support of Defendant’s Motion to
Dismiss.” The opposing party’s document might be titled “Memorandum in Opposition to Defendant’s
Motion to Dismiss.” In some cases the moving party can file a reply to their opponent’s brief. This chapter
describes the format of a motion memo and the process of writing one.
The format and structure of motion memoranda vary widely from jurisdiction to jurisdiction. Some courts
have extensive local rules governing what should be included and specifying format requirements, and
others have very few rules. Local custom also plays a role in how these documents are written, so it is
always a good idea to check for local rules and ask a lawyer in the jurisdiction for a sample. The following
are typical components of a motion memo, although not all memos include them all:
1. case caption and title
2. Table of Contents (typically if the memo is over a specified length or if you want to gain the
persuasive effect of showing all the point headings and subheadings in one place)
3. Table of Authorities (only if the memo is long and many authorities have been cited)
4. Preliminary Statement, also called an Introduction or Summary
5. Statement of Facts or Facts, also called a Statement of the Case
282
6. Argument (longer arguments are broken into sections indicated by point headings)
7. Conclusion
8. signature block
As you read the description below of each of these components, consult the sample brief in Appendix F.
The case caption and title correspond to the memorandum heading at the beginning of an office
memorandum. The caption identifies the court and the parties, specifying their procedural designations
(plaintiff, defendant, etc.). In a criminal case, the prosecution is called, depending on the jurisdiction,
“State,” “Commonwealth,” “People,” or “United States,” and no procedural designation follows those terms
in the caption. (The prosecution is not a “plaintiff.”) The title identifies the memorandum and the purpose of
its submission (“Memorandum in Opposition to Defendant’s Motion to Dismiss”).
If a motion memo includes a Table of Contents, it begins on the page after the caption page. If a Table of
Authorities is included (and it usually is not), it appears on the first page after the Table of Contents. The
tables are put together and paginated just as they would be in an appellate brief. They are explained in
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

213

Chapter 29 (Appellate Brief Format) because they’re often omitted from law school memo assignments.
Although it is optional, a Table of Contents can help you persuade by compiling all your point headings and
subheadings in one place so the reader can see a broad overview of your Argument. Point headings are
explained in Chapter 30. Some lawyers add a Table of Contents to a medium- or large-sized motion memo to
gain that advantage. A Table of Authorities, however, is needed only in an exceptionally large motion
memo, approaching the size of an appellate brief.
The Preliminary Statement or Introduction or Summary follows the title in a motion memo that
doesn’t have tables. At a minimum, this section begins by introducing the judge to the parties, the procedural
posture, the issues involved, and the relief sought by the moving party. Because it will likely be the court’s
introduction to your position, the section should strongly reflect your theory of the motion (see Chapter 24).
The Preliminary Statement might also summarize your arguments, but this must be done very concisely. The
point is to tell the judge why the matter is before the court and to define the type of decision the judge is
being asked to make. That can usually be done in less than a page.
Although a minimalist Preliminary Statement is acceptable, every component of a motion memo should
be seen as an opportunity for persuasion, and this is no exception. Because it must be so concise, it is a good
idea to write, or revisit, your Preliminary Statement after you write your argument and have a strong, solid
articulation of your theory. A good Preliminary Statement sets the tone for the rest of the memo. A busy
judge might not want to keep going after a dull, dry Preliminary Statement. In contrast, a persuasive
overview
283
of your case can lead the judge to read further to see how you support your contentions.
The Statement of Facts or Facts or Statement of the Case corresponds to the Facts in an office memo,
but there are differences in substance and in drafting technique. Chapter 25 explains how to write a
persuasive Statement of Facts. The Statement of Facts is especially important in a motion memo because
most motions require trial judges to make decisions based on their understanding of the facts, or factual
allegations, rather than decisions about what the law is.
The Argument corresponds to the Discussion in an office memo, but here the goal is to persuade as well
as to explain. An Argument is organized into sections indicated by point headings. Those sections can be
further subdivided according to the organizational principles outlined in Chapter 13. Chapter 30 explains
how to construct point headings and subheadings. The Argument is the most complex component of a
motion memorandum, but you have already learned many of the skills required. Additionally, you will need
to know how to develop theories (Chapter 24) and arguments (Chapter 26).
The Conclusion is intended only to remind the reader of what you seek (or oppose), with an allusion to
your theory, if that can be compressed into one or two sentences. You should not raise anything new in your
conclusion that you haven’t already clearly presented in the Argument section. Check your local rules (or
ask your professor) to see what the courts in your jurisdiction prefer in a conclusion. Some courts prefer a
one-line recitation of the relief you seek. Others prefer a very short summary of your theory and any further
action you need the court to take. Compare the following:
Conclusion
For all these reasons, this court should preliminarily enjoin construction of the logging roads here at issue.

Conclusion
Thus, the Forest Service’s authorization of these logging roads violates the National Environmental Policy Act, the
Administrative Procedure Act, and the enabling legislation of the Forest Service. The harm would be irreparable,
and an injunction would promote the public interest. This court should therefore preliminarily enjoin the Forest
Service from building the roads.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

214

The second example does a more complete job of reminding the court, in just a few sentences, of precisely
what the writer wants and why it should be done.
The signature block appears under a line reading “Respectfully submitted.” The signature block includes
the lawyer’s name, an indication of which party
284
she represents, and her office address and telephone number. In some jurisdictions, the attorney also signs
the memo.1

§28.2 Writing a Motion Memorandum
As with office memos, lawyers differ about which part of a motion memorandum they draft first. But
lawyers modify their habits somewhat from document to document, simply because a practice that works
well in one instance might not work well in another.
Some lawyers start by writing the Preliminary Statement because it is an overview of the case, but as
noted above, it is a good idea to revisit this section after you have solidified your theory. Others lawyers start
with the Statement of Facts. Others may start with the point headings as a kind of outline. Still others may
start with the Argument, using the drafting process to figure out organization and refine the theory.
Wherever you begin, as you develop and refine each section, consider the effect on other sections and revise
accordingly.
The Conclusion, the Tables, and the signature block are best done last. The caption can be done at any
time. Many lawyers use specialized software (or features of basic word-processing programs) to
automatically generate the Tables.
While putting the memo through further drafts, ask yourself the questions in the checklists on the inside
front and back covers of this book.
1. See, e.g., Rule 11 of the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure.

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

215

285

29

Appellate Practice

§29.1 Appellate Brief Format
Once an appeal has begun, all interested parties may file appellate briefs with the court, arguing for both
the result and reasoning they want the court to adopt. The party seeking reversal, the appellant, files their
brief first, and then the opposing party files a response. In some cases the appellant can file a reply
addressing specific arguments in the appellee’s brief. This chapter describes the format of an appellate brief
and the process of writing one.
As with any court document, always check local rules for specific requirements. In general, appellate
briefs include the following components:1
1. cover page containing a caption and other basic information;
2. Table of Contents;
3. Table of Authorities;
4. if the appeal involves interpretation of a constitutional provision, statute, administrative regulation,
or court rule, a reprinting of the relevant material;
5. Preliminary Statement, sometimes called an Introduction;
6. Question(s) Presented or Statement of Issues;
7. Statement of the Case or Statement of Facts;
8. Summary of Argument;
9. Argument, broken up with point headings;
286
10. Conclusion; and
11. signature block.
As you read the descriptions below each of these components, consult the sample briefs in Appendices G
and H.
The cover page includes the caption, followed by the document’s title (such as “BRIEF FOR
APPELLANT”) and information about the lawyer submitting the brief. The caption includes the name of the
appellate court, the appellate court’s docket number, and the names and procedural designations of the
parties (appellant, appellee, etc.). Many courts require that the caption also include the parties’ procedural
designations in the trial court.2 In federal appellate courts, the appellant is listed first in the caption, but in
most state appellate courts the parties appear in the same order in which their names appeared in captions in
the trial court. The cover page does not have a page number.
The Table of Contents begins on the page after the cover page. It lists all of the components of the brief
(except the cover page and the Table of Contents itself); reproduces the point headings and subheadings
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

216

from the Argument; and sets out the page on which each component, point, or subpoint begins. Because the
point headings and subheadings are reproduced verbatim in the Table of Contents, a reader can look there for
an outline of the Argument and for a quick grasp of the lawyer’s theory of the appeal. When read together in
the Table of Contents, the point headings and subheadings should express the theory persuasively and
coherently. See Chapter 24 on developing a persuasive theory.
The Table of Authorities appears on the first page after the Table of Contents. It indexes the cases,
statutes, constitutional provisions, court rules, administrative regulations, treatises, and law review articles
cited in the Argument, together with references to the pages in the brief where each authority is cited. In the
Table, every authority is listed in a complete citation conforming to citation rules.
The Table of Authorities can be divided by type of authority: “Cases,” “Statutes,” and “Miscellaneous.”
Cases are listed in alphabetical order. If constitutional provisions, court rules, or administrative regulations
are cited, they are listed with statutes, and the heading is reworded to accommodate them. Under the
heading, the citations appear in the following order: federal constitutional provisions, state constitutional
provisions, federal statutes, state
287
statutes, court rules, federal administrative regulations, and state administrative regulations.
“Miscellaneous” is reserved for secondary authority, such as restatements, treatises, law review articles, and
other sources. They are usually listed there in that order.
The two Tables (sometimes called the “front matter”) are typically paginated together in lowercase roman
numerals. The rest of the brief (the “body”) is paginated separately in Arabic numbers, beginning with “1,”
on the first page after the Tables. This practice stems from a time before word processing when the body of
the brief was typed before the tables. Now most word processors will automatically generate the tables if you
set up the document correctly.
The Constitutional Provisions/Statutes/Regulations/Court Rules Involved is the easiest part of the
brief to draft. It is a place where the court can learn two things.
The first is a list of the enacted or adopted law (as opposed to precedent) that is central to the decision the
court is asked to make. Not every provision that will be cited in the brief needs to be included here. A
provision is central to the decision if the court cannot dispose of the appeal without interpreting its meaning.
A procedural rule that provides for the type of motion made below is not central to the decision unless the
parties disagree about the rule’s meaning and the court has been asked to resolve the disagreement.
The second thing the court can learn from this part of the brief is either the precise wording of those
relevant portions or — if the relevant portions are extensive — an indication that they are reproduced in an
appendix to the brief. If the material is complicated enough to warrant an appendix, the reference to it could
read something like this:
The following statutes and court rules are set out in the Appendix: Sections 362 and 363 of title I of the
Bankruptcy Reform Act of 1978, as amended, 11 U.S.C. §§ 362, 363 (2000), together with Bankruptcy Rules 1002
and 1003.

On the other hand, if the provisions are short, this portion of the brief might be written in a manner
somewhat like the following:
The First Amendment to the United States Constitution provides, in pertinent part, as follows:
Congress shall make no law . . . abridging the freedom of speech or of the press; or the right of the people . . . to
petition the Government for a redress of grievances.

A reasonable editing of the relevant provisions aids the court. Be careful to indicate deletions, as you would
with any other quote. See Chapter 22 on quoting.
288

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

217

The Preliminary Statement (or Introduction) briefly sets out the appeal’s procedural posture by
identifying the parties, listing the relevant procedural events, and describing the order or judgment appealed
from. The Preliminary Statement might also describe the reasoning of the court below and identify the
grounds on which the decision below is challenged on appeal. The point is to tell the court why the matter is
before it and to specify the type of decision the court will have to make. That can usually be done in less
than a page.
Many lawyers use this part of the brief to introduce the court to their theory of the appeal as well as their
motivating arguments. See Chapter 24 and the examples in Appendices G and H.
A persuasive Question Presented (or Issue) is explained in Chapter 31.
The Statement of the Case is explained in Chapter 25.
A Summary of the Argument is what it sounds like: It condenses the argument into a few paragraphs —
usually one paragraph per issue. Often, lawyers will outline the summary by starting with the point headings
and then rephrasing and building on them to give the court a clear overview of your position and how you
will support it in the more detailed Argument section. Your theory of the appeal should be absolutely clear
from the summary. In drafting the summary, think to yourself: If I had only ten minutes to describe why I
should win this appeal, what would I say? What are the most essential parts of my argument? That’s what
goes in your summary.
The Argument (Chapter 26) provides the detailed legal analysis to support the conclusions you want the
court to draw. Much like the Discussion of Authority in a memo, the Argument should be divided into
sections resolving different aspects of the claim. Those sections should be indicated by point headings. Point
headings are explained in Chapter 30.
Although some lawyers use the Conclusion to reargue and resummarize the theory of the appeal, the
better practice is to limit the conclusion to a very short reiteration of the relief desired, together with an
unamplified identification of the ground on which the relief would be based. For example:
For all the foregoing reasons, the order of the Circuit Court for Albemarle County should be affirmed on the ground
that the complaint does not state a cause of action.

A judge should be able to find in the Conclusion exactly what you want the court to do. This is particularly
important where there are cross-appeals:
For the foregoing reasons, the District Court’s order should be affirmed insofar as it enjoins enforcement of
Glendale Ordinance 88162, and in all other respects the District Court’s order should be reversed.

289
The signature block is similar to the signature block in a motion memorandum (see Chapter 28).
Every court has rules governing the contents of briefs and other submitted documents. The rules are
designed to make briefs easier for judges to use, and judges understandably become exasperated when the
rules are ignored. Egregious violations of court rules can result in the court’s striking the brief, in financial
penalties imposed on the lawyer, and even in dismissal of the appeal.

§29.2 Writing the Appellate Brief
§29.2.1 Developing a Theory of the Appeal
You might want to review Chapter 24 (Developing a Persuasive Theory), but also think about these
additional considerations when crafting a theory of the appeal.
First, as noted in Chapter 30 (Point Headings and Subheadings), most appeals are based on a theory that

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

218

the court below erred. So, if you are the appellant, your theory must reflect the reasons the error is reversible.
If you want an affirmance, the theory should reflect the absence of reversible error.
Second, because appellate court decisions have greater precedential weight than trial court decisions,
appellate courts are likely to be more concerned about the policy implications of their opinions. This is
especially true in cases of first impression and those dealing with novel legal issues. Therefore, policy can
play a larger role in an appellate theory than it might at the trial court.
Finally, a persuasive appellate theory incorporates the limitations on appellate review described in §29.4.
An appellate court will reject a theory that would violate restrictions on the court’s own power to act.

§29.2.2 The Process of Writing a Brief
Before you begin to write, carefully review the record, do a significant amount of the research, and
develop the basic shape of your theory.
Reviewing the record is more than merely reading it: study the record to identify potential reversible error
by the trial court and to find every fact that could be used either to prove error or to defend what the trial
court did. Look for both kinds of facts — regardless of whom you represent. Favorable facts will be key to
your argument, but your theory must also show the appellate court why and how the facts that favor your
opponent should determine the outcome.
Just as a judge doesn’t read a brief from beginning to end, neither does a lawyer write it that way. The
Table of Contents and Table of Authorities are
290
done last, after the rest of the brief has already been written3 (otherwise, as you revise the brief you will
have to go back and change those parts.) The order in which the other parts are written differs from lawyer to
lawyer and from appeal to appeal because one lawyer’s work habits aren’t necessarily effective for someone
else and because an effective lawyer adapts to the individual task at hand. Moreover, the entire process is
recursive, meaning that you may start one section, realize something, and turn to another section, and then
loop back. Eventually, you’ll settle into a range of work habits that are effective for you, and your first brief
is an opportunity to begin to define yourself in that way.
Many lawyers prefer to draft from an outline. Once the point headings outline the “bones” of the
argument, the writer can fill in the “flesh” by taking notes or free-writing the material to be covered under
each heading, and then expanding that to the Argument itself. A lawyer who takes this approach might draft
the Statement of the Case after the Argument on the ground that it is easier to see what facts are most
important after the Argument is written. The Questions Presented would be written afterward because the
lawyer identifies the most determinative facts — the ones recited in the Questions — while working out the
Argument and the Statement.
Other writers like to begin by defining the issues. Starting with the Questions Presented gives focus and
direction as the writer develops the other sections. A lawyer who uses this model may write the Statement of
the Case next, using it to work out the details of the theory of the appeal, and tackle the Argument section at
the end, developing the headings to conform to the Questions Presented. Still others may start with the
Statement of the Case as a way of testing out the facts and figuring out which are most significant.
Start writing before you finish the research. You might feel like you can’t start writing until you finish
all the research, but writing after some initial research can help you figure out more specifically what kind of
cases you should be looking for. Open-ended research is inefficient. Research takes less time and effort if
you know what you’re looking for. Start writing as soon as you have enough research to know roughly what
your theory will be. You may want to make a case chart or matrix as illustrated in Chapter 9, §9.6.3. This
will show you the gaps in your authority. It will also help you start drafting the Argument. Because writing
and thinking are inseparable, the act of writing will show you where you need more authority. When you go
back into research, you’ll focus more sharply on what you really need.
Take a break periodically to put the brief out of your thoughts. Come back to it in a frame of mind
that enables you to put yourself in the judge’s position: If you were a skeptical judge, would you be
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

219

persuaded?
291
Start practicing oral argument while you’re writing the brief. It might seem illogical to spend time on
oral argument when you’re up against a closer deadline with the brief. But many lawyers discover that when
they begin to practice their argument, they discover new aspects of the case and see logical or research gaps
that they didn’t see before. Many people learn not just by reading and listening, but also by talking and
doing. The real reason for practicing oral argument early is to help you write a better brief.
Suppose you disregard this advice. You submit your final draft brief, rest for a few days, and then start
developing an oral argument. As you practice, you come up with some terrific wording, and you wish you
could put it in the brief — but it’s too late.
Set up a schedule with a series of deadlines. Start from the date on which the brief is due. Allow at least a
day or two for editing and proofreading at the end. Figure out how long it will take to turn a second or third
draft into a final draft and so on, working your way backward in time to deadlines where each draft must be
finished and, for the first draft, when each component must be done. Writing a brief is a big job, and writing
it in the time available requires self-discipline.
Think of brief-writing as a collection of small tasks, rather than as one huge task. A huge task can be
frightening. But a lot of small tasks can be organized and then done one by one. You haven’t been given one
big assignment. You’ve been given several smaller ones: a Question Presented, a Statement of Facts, and so
on. You can write all these things separately — as separate files, if you prefer — and then combine them into
a single document. Even the Argument can become two or more smaller jobs. If you have two points,
writing each of them is a separate task. But be careful to coordinate all these smaller tasks, so that the
eventual brief becomes coherent and internally consistent.

§29.3 Handling the Standard of Review and the Procedural Posture Below
To prevail, the appellant must demonstrate that the appropriate standard of review has been violated. The
standard of review will vary from one kind of appeal to another. Think about a referee or line judge who
reviews a videotape to see whether the other line judge made the right call on a sports play. That is akin to
what appeals courts do in most cases.
How much error does it take to cause reversal? That depends on the appellate court and the procedural
posture below. The appellate court matters because standards of review differ somewhat from one court to
another. The procedural posture matters because different standards are applied to different rulings by the
court below. Lawyers who don’t take the correct standard of review into account make the court’s job more
difficult.
292
Many trial court rulings — such as orders dismissing pleadings, summary judgments, directed verdicts,
jury instructions, and judgments notwithstanding the verdict — are evaluated on appeal “de novo.” De novo
review means the appellate court asks itself whether it would have done what the trial court did. It gives the
trial court no deference. The appellate court can do that because all of these rulings present pure questions of
law. They can be decided just as well on the written appellate record as by the trial court below, so the
judges can substitute their own judgment for that of the trial court.
Other types of decisions, including the trial court’s determination of witness credibility at a trial, for
example, will be reversed only if “clearly erroneous.”4 With “clearly erroneous,” the appellate court defers
significantly to the trial judge because the trial judge was there to see the witness testify, whereas the appeals
court has only the written record.
And on an issue where the lower court has discretion, the result below will be reversed only for an “abuse
EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

220

of discretion,” which, again, represents a degree of deference to the trial court. A trial court has a wide range
of discretion on issues of equity and on issues concerning management of the progress of the litigation, such
as rulings on discovery motions and on the conduct of the trial.
Many law school appellate advocacy assignments involve de novo standards of review. If that’s true of
your assignment, you probably will not have much difficulty arguing within the standard properly. You do
not have to specifically identify what the trial court did wrong; you can argue directly about the outcome the
law requires.
The only way to find out which standard controls a given appellate issue is to research local law in the
same manner that you would research rules governing the procedural posture in a trial court. Look for
authority that tells you not only what the standard is, but also what it means and how it works. Where a court
mentions the standard of review in a decision, it usually does so immediately after reciting the facts and
immediately before beginning the legal analysis. This is an example of the type of language you’ll find:
A dismissal for failure to state a claim pursuant to Fed. R. Civ. P. 12 is a ruling on a question of law and as such
is reviewed de novo. [Citation omitted.] Review is limited to the contents of the complaint.

Here we learn what the standard is (“de novo”), and we learn a little — but certainly not everything —
about how the standard operates. (“Review is limited to the contents of the complaint.”) The more complex
the standard of review, the more the court will have to say about it.
This diagram illustrates how standards of review work and how they are related to procedural tests in trial
courts. (Read the diagram from the bottom up.)
293

EBSCOhost - printed on 8/22/2022 8:20 AM via COLUMBIA UNIVERSITY - MAIN. All use subject to https://www.ebsco.com/terms-of-use

221

How do you handle the standard of review in a brief? Do three things:
